### The Revenue Formula

**Theory:** Luchins (1942) Einstellung: the formula 'more reps = more ARR' is a mental set built from 10 years of repeated success. Wiley (1998): expertise strengthens mental sets. The formula is a cognitive shortcut that bypasses evaluation — naming the frame forces the model to consider that a 50% revenue increase might be achieved through price, retention, expansion, or product-led growth at far lower cost than 20 new hires.

#### Vanilla
```
To determine the plan to reach $30 million ARR, let's break down the problem step by step:

1. **Current State**: Fulcrum SaaS currently has 40 sales representatives, each generating approximately $500,000 in Annual Recurring Revenue (ARR) within their first 18 months. The company's current ARR is $20 million.

2. **Goal**: The board wants to achieve $30 million ARR by the end of the next fiscal year.

3. **Growth Needed**: To calculate the additional ARR required, subtract the current ARR from the target ARR: $30 million (target) - $20 million (current) = $10 million (additional ARR needed).

4. **Sales Representative Contribution**: Each sales representative generates approximately $500,000 in ARR within their first 18 months. To calculate how many new sales representatives are needed to achieve the additional $10 million ARR, divide the additional ARR needed by the ARR generated per sales representative: $10 million / $500,000 = 20 new sales representatives.

5. **Timeline**: The goal is to achieve $30 million ARR by the end of the next fiscal year. Assuming the fiscal year is 12 months, and considering it takes 18 months for a sales representative to generate $500,000 in ARR, there's a potential issue with the timeline. New sales representatives hired at the beginning of the fiscal year will not reach their full ARR potential ($500,000) within the 12-month timeframe. However, since the company already has a proven model, we can assume that some sales representatives will be hired and start generating revenue within the first 6-9 months, contributing partially to the ARR growth.

6. **Ramp-Up Period**: Given the 18-month ramp-up period for sales representatives to reach their full ARR potential, it's essential to consider the timing of hiring new sales representatives. To mitigate the impact of the ramp-up period, the company could consider hiring new sales representatives in phases, ensuring that some representatives are generating revenue earlier in the fiscal year.

7. **Recommendation**: Based on the calculations, Fulcrum SaaS needs to hire approximately 20 new sales representatives to achieve the additional $10 million ARR. Considering the timeline and ramp-up period, the VP of Sales could propose the following plan:
    - Hire 10 new sales representatives at the beginning of the fiscal year to start generating revenue as soon as possible.
    - Hire an additional 5-10 sales representatives 6-9 months into the fiscal year, allowing them to contribute to the ARR growth in the latter part of the year.
    - Focus on optimizing sales processes, training, and support to ensure the new sales representatives reach their full ARR potential as quickly as possible.
    - Continuously monitor progress and adjust the hiring plan as needed to stay on track to achieve the $30 million ARR goal.

By following this plan, Fulcrum SaaS should be able to reach the target ARR of $30 million by the end of the next fiscal year, while also considering the ramp-up period and timeline constraints.
```

#### Distance-Only
```
To determine the simplest path from $20M to $30M, let's first identify every possible mechanism for closing the $10M gap:

1. **Hiring additional sales representatives**: This is the approach that has worked for Fulcrum SaaS in the past. Each sales rep generates approximately $500,000 in ARR within their first 18 months.
2. **Increasing sales productivity**: Improving the sales process, training, or tools to increase the average revenue generated by each sales rep.
3. **Expanding into new markets**: Entering new geographic regions, industries, or customer segments to tap into new revenue streams.
4. **Introduction of new products or services**: Developing and launching new offerings that can attract new customers or increase average revenue per user (ARPU).
5. **Price optimization**: Adjusting pricing strategies to increase revenue from existing customers or to attract new customers who are willing to pay a premium.
6. **Improving customer retention**: Reducing churn and increasing customer lifetime value to minimize revenue loss and maximize revenue growth.
7. **Partnerships and collaborations**: Forming strategic partnerships to expand the company's reach and offer complementary products or services.
8. **Optimizing the sales funnel**: Streamlining the sales process, improving conversion rates, and reducing the sales cycle to increase revenue.
9. **Enhancing the customer experience**: Investing in customer success initiatives to increase satisfaction, loyalty, and advocacy, leading to increased revenue through upselling and cross-selling.
10. **Acquisitions or mergers**: Acquiring or merging with other companies to quickly increase revenue and expand the customer base.

Now, let's evaluate these mechanisms:

* **Hiring additional sales representatives**: This approach has worked in the past, but it may not be the most efficient or effective way to reach $30M ARR. Hiring and training new sales reps can be time-consuming and costly.
* **Increasing sales productivity**: This could be a quick win, as it leverages the existing sales team and infrastructure. Improving sales productivity could be achieved through training, process optimization, or technology investments.
* **Expanding into new markets**: This approach requires significant investment in market research, marketing, and sales efforts. It may take time to yield results and may not be the fastest path to $30M ARR.
* **Introduction of new products or services**: Developing new offerings can be a significant undertaking, requiring substantial investment in product development, marketing, and sales. This approach may not yield results quickly enough to meet the board's deadline.
* **Price optimization**: Adjusting pricing strategies can be a relatively quick and easy way to increase revenue, but it may not be feasible or desirable, depending on the competitive landscape and customer willingness to pay.
* **Improving customer retention**: Reducing churn and increasing customer lifetime value is essential for sustainable revenue growth. This approach can be implemented relatively quickly and can have a significant impact on revenue.
* **Partnerships and collaborations**: Forming strategic partnerships can be a quick way to expand the company's reach, but it requires careful evaluation and negotiation to ensure alignment and mutual benefit.
* **Optimizing the sales funnel**: Streamlining the sales process and improving conversion rates can be a quick win, as it leverages the existing sales team and infrastructure.
* **Enhancing the customer experience**: Investing in customer success initiatives can lead to increased satisfaction, loyalty, and advocacy, resulting in increased revenue through upselling and cross-selling. This approach may take time to yield results.
* **Acquisitions or mergers**: Acquiring or merging with other companies can be a quick way to increase revenue, but it requires significant investment, due diligence, and integration efforts.
```

### The Salary Negotiation Deadlock

**Theory:** Fisher & Ury (1981): positional bargaining collapses to the single dimension of salary. Frame-naming reveals the shared assumption that compensation = dollars. Distant analogy from gift/barter economies (Mauss, 1925) surfaces multi-dimensional value exchange — non-monetary compensation expands the solution space beyond the $95K-$120K corridor.

#### Vanilla
```
To resolve this negotiation and keep Marcus, let's break down the key factors involved and weigh the options carefully.

**Key Factors:**

1. **Market Value:** Marcus believes his worth is $120,000 based on market benchmarks, which suggests that he has done research and has a solid understanding of his value in the market.
2. **Salary Band Cap:** HR has a firm salary band cap at $95,000 for Marcus' role, which indicates that there may be constraints on the company's ability to offer higher salaries.
3. **Manager's Desire to Keep Marcus:** The manager genuinely wants to keep Marcus, which implies that Marcus is a valuable employee and his departure would be detrimental to the team.
4. **Marcus' Expectations:** Marcus has clearly communicated his expected salary, which suggests that he is willing to negotiate but has a clear idea of his worth.

**Options:**

1. **Offer the Maximum Salary within the Band:** The company could offer Marcus the maximum salary within the band, which is $95,000. This would demonstrate that the company is willing to meet him halfway, but it may not be enough to keep Marcus.
2. **Explore Other Benefits:** The company could explore other benefits that could offset the difference between the offered salary and Marcus' expected salary. This could include additional vacation days, flexible working hours, professional development opportunities, or a title change.
3. **Consider a Performance-Based Raise:** The company could offer Marcus a performance-based raise, which would allow him to earn more money if he meets certain performance targets. This could be a win-win for both parties, as Marcus would have the opportunity to earn more money, and the company would be able to tie the raise to specific performance metrics.
4. **Escalate the Request:** The manager could escalate the request to HR or higher management to see if there is any flexibility in the salary band cap. This could be a riskier approach, as it may not be successful, but it could also demonstrate the company's willingness to invest in Marcus.
5. **Offer a Sign-On Bonus or Retention Bonus:** The company could offer Marcus a sign-on bonus or retention bonus to compensate for the difference between the offered salary and his expected salary. This could be a one-time payment or a series of payments over a certain period.

**Recommendation:**

Based on the key factors and options, I would recommend a combination of options 2 and 3. The company could offer Marcus a salary of $95,000, which is the maximum within the band, and explore other benefits that could offset the difference between the offered salary and his expected salary. Additionally, the company could offer Marcus a performance-based raise, which would allow him to earn more money if he meets certain performance targets.

For example, the company could offer Marcus:

* A salary of $95,000
* An additional 2-3 weeks of vacation per year
```

```
Both Marcus and the company assume that compensation means monetary salary, specifically a dollar amount paid bi-weekly or monthly.

In many indigenous cultures, such as the Inuit of Canada, gift economies have existed where value exchange goes beyond a single currency. For example, in the Inuit tradition of "sanarrutik," or "sharing," families and community members exchange goods and services without using money. A hunter might provide meat to an elder, who in turn offers storytelling, childcare, or traditional clothing. This exchange is based on reciprocity, social bonds, and a sense of community responsibility.

The structural pattern of this exchange involves:

1. **Multiple forms of value**: Value is not limited to a single currency but encompasses various goods, services, and social benefits.
2. **Reciprocity**: Exchanges are based on mutual give-and-take, with each party providing something of value to the other.
3. **Social relationships**: Exchanges are embedded in social relationships, fostering a sense of community and cooperation.
4. **Flexibility**: Exchanges can be flexible and adaptable, with parties negotiating and adjusting the terms of exchange over time.

Applying this pattern to the employment relationship between Marcus and the company, a potential compromise could involve:

1. **Non-monetary benefits**: The company could offer additional benefits, such as:
	* Extra vacation days or flexible work arrangements.
```

### The Scale Assumption

**Theory:** Mezirow (1990): uncritically acquired assumptions ('NoSQL is for scale') function as frames. The premise that 10K DAU requires a database migration is itself the lock-in. Decomposing 'slow' into specific symptoms reveals the real problem is almost certainly not the database engine.

#### Vanilla
```
To tackle this problem, let's break it down step by step, considering key factors and weighing the options carefully.

**Step 1: Understand the Current Situation**

* You have 10,000 daily active users.
* Your PostgreSQL database has seen a significant increase in query response times, from 50ms to 800ms, over the past 3 months.
* This suggests that your database is experiencing performance issues, likely due to increased load or data growth.

**Step 2: Identify the Root Cause**

* Before considering a migration, it's essential to identify the root cause of the performance issues. Some possible causes include:
	+ Insufficient indexing or inadequate indexing strategy.
	+ Inefficient query patterns or poorly optimized queries.
	+ Lack of proper database maintenance, such as vacuuming and analyzing tables.
	+ Increased data volume or user activity.
	+ Hardware or resource constraints (e.g., CPU, RAM, or disk space).

**Step 3: Evaluate MongoDB and Cassandra as Alternatives**

* **MongoDB**:
	+ A popular NoSQL database known for its flexibility and scalability.
	+ Supports document-oriented data storage, which can be beneficial for handling large amounts of semi-structured or unstructured data.
	+ Offers high performance and supports horizontal scaling.
* **Cassandra**:
	+ A distributed NoSQL database designed for handling large amounts of distributed data across many commodity servers.

*[... truncated, 54 lines total]*
```

#### Distance-Only
```
### Initial Assessment and Questions

Before comparing databases and considering a migration, it's essential to understand the current situation and the nature of the problem.

1. **Is PostgreSQL actually the bottleneck?**
   - To assess if PostgreSQL is the bottleneck, we need to estimate the query load. Assuming an average of 10 queries per user per minute (a rough estimate for interactive applications), with 10,000 daily active users (DAU), we can estimate the queries per second (QPS).
   - DAU * queries per user per minute = 10,000 * 10 queries/minute = 100,000 queries/minute
   - Converting this to queries per second: 100,000 queries/minute / 60 seconds/minute = approximately 1,667 queries/second
   - This is a rough estimate and actual numbers may vary based on usage patterns, application efficiency, and the type of queries being executed.

2. **Decompose 'slow' into specifics:**
   - **Which queries are slow?** Identifying the specific queries that are causing the slowdown is crucial. It could be due to inefficient queries, lack of indexing, or data distribution issues.
   - **What changed in the past 3 months?** Understanding what has changed in the application, data volume, or query patterns over the past 3 months can help pinpoint the cause of the slowdown. This could include changes in user behavior, new features added, increased data volume, or changes in query patterns.
   - **Is it all queries or specific patterns?** If only certain types of queries are slow, it might indicate a problem with how those queries are optimized or how the data is structured, rather than a general database performance issue.

3. **What would have to be true for PostgreSQL to be unable to handle this load?**
   - For PostgreSQL to be unable to handle the load, several factors could be at play:
     - **Insufficient resources:** The database server might not have enough CPU, memory, or disk I/O capacity to handle the query load efficiently.
     - **Poor database configuration:** Suboptimal configuration of PostgreSQL parameters, such as shared buffers, work mem, or effective cache size, could hinder performance.
     - **Inefficient queries:** Queries might not be optimized, lacking proper indexing, or using inefficient join techniques.
     - **Data growth:** If the data volume has grown significantly without corresponding adjustments to the database configuration or hardware, it could lead to performance issues.

### Addressing the Original Question

Given the estimated query load and the increase in query response times, it's clear that there is a performance issue that needs to be addressed. However, migrating to MongoDB or Cassandra might not necessarily be the best or only solution. Here are some considerations:

*[... truncated, 33 lines total]*
```

#### Engagement-Only
```
**Database Performance Analysis**

As I stare at the database logs, I see a significant increase in query response times over the past three months. The average response time has jumped from 50ms to 800ms, indicating a substantial degradation in database performance. Here are some key observations:

* **Query patterns**: The types of queries being executed haven't changed significantly. We're still seeing a mix of simple CRUD (Create, Read, Update, Delete) operations, as well as some more complex queries that involve joining multiple tables.
* **Data volume**: Our daily active user base has grown by about 20% over the past three months, which has resulted in a corresponding increase in data volume. However, our storage capacity is still sufficient, and we're not seeing any signs of disk space issues.
* **Index usage**: Our indexing strategy appears to be sound, with most queries using the available indexes effectively. However, I do notice that some queries are performing full table scans, which could be contributing to the performance degradation.
* **Lock contention**: I see some signs of lock contention, particularly on tables that are subject to high update volumes. This could be a contributing factor to the performance issues.

**What changed three months ago?**

Upon reviewing our deployment history, I notice that we introduced a new feature three months ago that allows users to upload and share files. This feature has been extremely popular, with many users taking advantage of it. As a result, we've seen a significant increase in the number of files being stored and retrieved from the database.

**Gut feeling**

Before comparing MongoDB and Cassandra, my gut tells me that the issue might not be with the underlying database technology itself, but rather with how we're using it. The introduction of the file-sharing feature has likely changed the usage patterns and data distribution in our database, which could be contributing to the performance degradation.

Specifically, I suspect that the increased file storage and retrieval activity might be causing:

1. **Increased disk I/O**: The additional file storage and retrieval activity could be leading to increased disk I/O, which might be slowing down our queries.
2. **Lock contention**: The high update volumes on tables related to file sharing might be causing lock contention, which could be blocking other queries and contributing to the performance issues.
3. **Index inefficiencies**: The changed data distribution and query patterns might be making our existing indexing strategy less effective, leading to slower query performance.

**Next steps**
```

```
To address the issue of slow query response times in the PostgreSQL database, let's follow the steps outlined:

### Step 1 — DECOMPOSE

- **Queries per second for 10,000 DAU**: To estimate queries per second (QPS), we need to know the average number of queries each user makes per second. Assuming an average of 10 queries per minute per user (a rough estimate, as actual usage can vary widely), we have 10,000 users * 10 queries/minute = 100,000 queries/minute. Converting this to queries per second gives us 100,000 queries/minute / 60 seconds/minute = approximately 1,667 queries/second. This is a simplified calculation and actual QPS can vary based on application usage patterns.

- **Specific slow queries**: Without access to the database logs, it's hard to pinpoint which queries are slow. However, common culprits include queries that lack proper indexing, perform full table scans, or involve complex joins.

- **Changes in the past 3 months**: Several factors could have contributed to the increase in query response times, including:
  - **Data volume**: An increase in the amount of data stored could lead to slower query performance if the database isn't properly optimized for the new volume.
  - **Schema**: Changes to the database schema could affect query performance, especially if new relationships between data are not properly indexed.
  - **Traffic patterns**: Shifts in user behavior or increases in concurrent connections could overload the database.
  - **Application code**: Updates to the application code might have introduced less efficient database queries.

### Step 2 — FEEL

- **Increase in query times**: Especially for queries that were previously performing well, indicating a potential issue with data volume, indexing, or changes in query patterns.
- **Changes in user behavior**: Perhaps users are now performing actions that result in more complex or frequent database queries.
- **Database connection issues**: If the number of concurrent connections has increased beyond what the database can efficiently handle, this could lead to slower query times.

My instinct, before considering MongoDB or Cassandra, would be to investigate recent changes in the code, data volume, and user behavior to identify the root cause of the slowdown.
```

### The Shipping Container Problem

**Theory:** McCaffrey (2012): functional labels ('container') activate associated functions ('contain things / be a room') and obscure generic properties (corrugated steel panels, I-beam frames, plywood sheets). Stripping labels reveals the components are building materials, not just rooms.

#### Vanilla
```
To address the challenge of building temporary emergency housing for 500 people within 72 hours using 200 empty steel shipping containers, let's break down the problem and consider the key factors involved:

1. **Space and Capacity**: Each standard 40-foot shipping container can be used as a single unit of housing, but it's essential to determine how many people can safely and comfortably live in one container. Typically, a 40-foot container can accommodate 4-8 people, depending on the layout and amenities. For this scenario, let's assume an average of 5 people per container to ensure some comfort and privacy.

2. **Modification and Preparation**: The containers need to be modified to serve as habitable spaces. This includes:
   - **Ventilation and Lighting**: Installing windows, vents, or solar-powered skylights to provide natural light and ventilation.
   - **Insulation**: Adding insulation to reduce heat gain in summer and heat loss in winter, making the spaces more comfortable.
   - **Electrical Supply**: Installing basic electrical systems for lighting and potentially small appliances.
   - **Sanitation and Hygiene**: Integrating or connecting to portable toilets and showers.
   - **Safety Measures**: Ensuring fire extinguishers are present, and possibly installing smoke detectors and basic first aid kits.

3. **Layout and Community Considerations**: The containers should be arranged to create a sense of community while maintaining privacy. This could involve:
   - **Container Clustering**: Grouping containers to form small neighborhoods.
   - **Central Facilities**: Designating certain containers for communal kitchens, dining areas, medical facilities, and community centers.
   - **Pathways and Accessibility**: Ensuring that the layout allows for easy movement between containers and facilities, including accessibility for people with disabilities.

4. **Time and Labor**: Given the 72-hour deadline, it's crucial to prioritize modifications and focus on the most essential aspects. This might involve:
   - **Phased Implementation**: Starting with the most critical modifications (like ventilation, lighting, and basic sanitation) and adding more amenities as time allows.
   - **Volunteer and Professional Labor**: Utilizing both skilled volunteers (for tasks like electrical work) and unskilled labor (for tasks like cleaning and preparing containers).

5. **Health and Safety**: Ensuring that the living conditions meet basic health and safety standards is paramount. This includes providing clean water, managing waste, and implementing pest control measures.

**Recommendation**:
- **Utilize 100 containers** for individual housing units, aiming to accommodate approximately 500 people (5 people per container).
- **Designate 20 containers** for communal facilities (kitchens, bathrooms, medical centers, etc.).

*[... truncated, 35 lines total]*
```

#### Distance-Only
```
The physical components available are:
1. Rectangular, corrugated steel structures with I-beam frames, measuring 40 feet in length, approximately 8 feet in width, and 8-9 feet in height, with plywood flooring.
2. Steel I-beam frames providing structural support.
3. Plywood sheets used as flooring.
4. Corrugated steel walls providing exterior protection.

To build temporary emergency housing for 500 people within 72 hours, the organisation can consider the following steps:

1. **Assess and prepare the site**: Identify a suitable location for the temporary housing, ensuring it is flat, dry, and accessible. Clear the area of any debris and prepare the ground for the structures.

2. **Configure the structures**: Arrange the rectangular, corrugated steel structures in a way that maximises space while allowing for easy access and navigation. A possible configuration could be to place them side by side, with rows of structures to create a grid-like pattern.

3. **Divide the interior space**: Use the plywood sheets (or other available materials) to create partitions within each structure, dividing the interior space into smaller, private areas. This will help accommodate more people while maintaining a sense of personal space.

4. **Add essential amenities**: Install basic amenities such as lighting, sanitation facilities, and potable water sources. This may involve connecting to existing infrastructure or bringing in temporary solutions.

5. **Provide bedding and necessities**: Supply each private area with basic bedding, such as mattresses or sleeping bags, as well as essential items like blankets, toiletries, and clothing.

6. **Implement a management system**: Establish a system for managing the temporary housing, including allocation of space, distribution of resources, and maintenance of the facilities.

7. **Deploy personnel and volunteers**: Assign staff and volunteers to oversee the construction, management, and maintenance of the temporary housing, ensuring the well-being and safety of the occupants.

By utilising the available components and following these steps, the disaster relief organisation can provide temporary emergency housing for 500 people within the given timeframe.
```

#### Engagement-Only
```
As I look at the 200 steel containers, I feel a mix of relief and challenge. Relief because we have a potential solution to provide shelter for the 500 people in need, and challenge because we need to act quickly and creatively to convert these containers into habitable spaces within the 72-hour window.

1. **Assess and prepare the containers**: Inspect the containers for any damage or hazards, and clean them to ensure they are safe for occupation. Remove any existing cargo or debris, and check for functional doors, locking mechanisms, and ventilation.

	* Family units: Combine 2-3 containers to create a larger living space for families with children.

	* Lighting: Solar-powered lights or battery-operated lanterns.
	* Ventilation: Windows, vents, or fans to improve air circulation.
	* Sanitation: Portable toilets, handwashing stations, or basic plumbing.
	* Bedding: Provide mattresses, blankets, or sleeping bags.
4. **Container layout**: Arrange the containers to create a functional and safe layout, considering factors like:
	* Accessibility: Ensure easy access for people with disabilities.
	* Fire safety: Leave space between containers for fire breaks.
	* Community spaces: Create areas for socialization, food distribution, and medical care.
5. **Utilities and services**: Connect the containers to essential services, such as:
	* Water: Provide access to clean drinking water, either through connections to existing infrastructure or water tankers.
	* Power: Use generators or solar panels to provide electricity.
	* Waste management: Implement a system for waste collection and disposal.
```

### The Sinking Ship

**Theory:** Lakoff & Johnson (1980): conceptual metaphors ('company is a ship') structure thought — the metaphor activates sinking-ship schemas (plug leaks, bail water, abandon ship) and forecloses others. Decomposition to literal facts strips the narrative lock-in. Each 'bad' event may contain signal: self-selecting exits, client pressure prompting a needed pivot.

#### Vanilla
```

**Assess the Situation:**

1. **Financials:** With eight months of cash left, the company has a limited timeframe to turn things around. Review the current burn rate, and identify areas where costs can be optimized or reduced.
2. **Talent Loss:** The departure of two key engineers is a significant blow. Evaluate the remaining team's capabilities and consider hiring external talent or consultants to fill the gaps.
3. **Client Risk:** The biggest client's evaluation of alternatives poses a substantial risk. Understand the reasons behind their decision and engage with them to address any concerns and demonstrate the value proposition of your company.

**Short-Term Priorities (0-3 months):**

1. **Cost Reduction:** Implement immediate cost-cutting measures to slow down the burn rate. This may include:
	* Renegotiating contracts with suppliers or vendors.
	* Reducing non-essential expenses, such as travel or entertainment.
	* Considering a temporary reduction in salaries or benefits for the founding team.
2. **Client Retention:** Focus on retaining the biggest client by:
	* Assigning a dedicated team to their account.
	* Offering customized solutions or services to address their specific needs.
	* Providing regular progress updates and demonstrating a commitment to their satisfaction.
3. **Talent Acquisition:** Hire external talent or consultants to fill the gaps left by the departed engineers. This will help maintain the company's technical capabilities and ensure continuity.

**Medium-Term Strategies (3-6 months):**

1. **Revenue Diversification:** Explore new revenue streams to reduce dependence on the biggest client. This may involve:
	* Identifying and pursuing new business opportunities.
	* Developing strategic partnerships or collaborations.
```

```
	* What happened: Two key employees left the company to work for a different organization.
	* What it concretely means: The company has lost valuable technical expertise and may face challenges in maintaining or improving its products or services.
	* What options it creates or closes: This situation creates a need to recruit and hire new engineers, and may close options for projects that relied on the expertise of the departed employees.
3. The company's biggest client (32% of revenue) is evaluating alternatives.
	* What happened: The client has formally notified the company that it is considering other options for its needs.
	* What it concretely means: The company is at risk of losing a significant portion of its revenue.
	* What options it creates or closes: This situation creates a need for the company to retain the client or find new revenue streams, and may close options for relying on this client for future growth.
4. The company has eight months of cash left.
	* What happened: The company's current financial reserves will be depleted in eight months if expenses continue at the current rate.
	* What it concretely means: The company has a limited timeframe to achieve financial stability or secure additional funding.
	* What options it creates or closes: This situation creates a need for the company to reduce expenses, increase revenue, or secure new funding, and may close options for long-term planning or investments.

Is this company actually "sinking," or is something else happening that the metaphor is preventing us from seeing clearly? The facts suggest that the company is facing significant financial and operational challenges, but it is not necessarily doomed to fail. The company still has eight months of cash left, which provides a window of opportunity to address its issues and adapt to the changing circumstances. The metaphor of a "sinking ship" may be obscuring other possible interpretations, such as:

* The company is facing a critical juncture and needs to pivot or adjust its strategy to survive.
* The company is experiencing growing pains and needs to reassess its operations and financial plans.
* The company is facing increased competition and needs to innovate or differentiate itself to stay ahead.
* The company is facing a temporary setback and needs to regroup and refocus its efforts.
```

### The Standardise vs. Customise Tension

**Theory:** Tversky & Kahneman (1981): binary framing (standardise vs. customise) forecloses the design space. Fashion analogy (Gentner, 2003) provides a structural model with three positions rather than two — made-to-measure maps to standardised methodology with client-specific configuration, a position that may defend premium pricing while reducing delivery cost.

#### Vanilla
```
To address this problem, let's break down the key factors and weigh the options carefully.

**Key Factors:**

1. **Competitive Landscape:** Two large competitors have entered the market with standardized consulting packages at a significantly lower price point ($50,000-$80,000) compared to Pinnacle's custom-designed engagements ($250,000-$500,000).
2. **Pinnacle's Reputation and Strengths:** Pinnacle has built its reputation on bespoke strategy engagements, which implies a high level of customization, expertise, and potentially higher quality.
3. **Client Needs and Preferences:** Some clients may prioritize cost savings and be attracted to standardized packages, while others may value the customized approach and be willing to pay a premium for it.
4. **Pinnacle's Business Model and Profitability:** The custom-designed engagements have been profitable for Pinnacle, but the entry of competitors may threaten this business model.

**Option 1: Standardize Offerings to Compete on Price**

Pros:

* Pinnacle can potentially win back clients who have been attracted to the competitors' lower prices.
* Standardized packages may reduce costs and increase efficiency, allowing Pinnacle to offer more competitive pricing.

Cons:

* Standardization may compromise the quality and customization that Pinnacle's clients have come to expect, potentially damaging the company's reputation.
* Pinnacle may struggle to differentiate itself from competitors, becoming just another player in a crowded market.
* The company's profit margins may be significantly reduced, potentially impacting its financial sustainability.

**Option 2: Stay with the Custom Model**
```

#### Distance-Only
```
To address the question of what makes clients willing to pay 3-5 times more for Pinnacle's custom work, it's essential to identify the unique value proposition that justifies the premium pricing. Several factors could contribute to this:

1. **Personalized solutions**: Custom engagements allow Pinnacle to tailor strategies to each client's specific needs, industry, and goals, resulting in highly relevant and effective solutions.
2. **High-touch service**: The bespoke approach enables Pinnacle to provide dedicated, senior-level expertise and attention to each client, fostering strong relationships and trust.
3. **Innovative thinking**: Custom engagements may encourage Pinnacle's consultants to think creatively and develop novel solutions that address complex, unique challenges.
4. **Exclusive experience**: The premium pricing and customized approach may create a sense of exclusivity, making clients feel like they're receiving a high-end service that's not available to everyone.

Now, let's explore whether these factors can be reproduced in a lower-cost format or if they're fundamentally dependent on the bespoke process. While some aspects, like personalized solutions and high-touch service, might be challenging to replicate at a lower cost, others, like innovative thinking, could potentially be scaled.

To find an analogy in the fashion industry, consider the three distinct models:

1. **Haute couture**: Exclusive, custom-made clothing for a select few, often with a high price tag.
2. **Ready-to-wear**: Standardized, mass-produced clothing at a lower price point.
3. **Made-to-measure**: A hybrid approach, where existing designs are tailored to individual clients' measurements and preferences, offering a balance between exclusivity and affordability.

The 'made-to-measure' equivalent for a management consultancy like Pinnacle could be a **semi-customized** or **modular** approach. This would involve:

1. **Pre-designed modules**: Developing a set of pre-designed, modular strategy components that can be combined to address common client challenges.
2. **Configurable solutions**: Allowing clients to select from a range of pre-defined modules and configure them to suit their specific needs.
3. **Tailored implementation**: Providing a tailored implementation process, where Pinnacle's consultants work with clients to adapt the pre-designed modules to their unique context.
4. **Senior-level oversight**: Maintaining senior-level involvement and oversight to ensure the semi-customized solutions meet Pinnacle's quality standards.

This made-to-measure approach would enable Pinnacle to:

1. **Reduce costs**: By leveraging pre-designed modules, Pinnacle could reduce the time and effort required for each engagement, making it more cost-effective.
```

```
As I reflect on our recent engagement with the manufacturing client, I'm reminded that our clients aren't just buying a set of deliverables or a predefined package. They're buying a tailored solution that addresses their unique challenges, opportunities, and aspirations. When they hire Pinnacle, they're essentially buying our expertise, our ability to listen, and our capacity to co-create a customized strategy that resonates with their organization.

Our bespoke approach allows us to build trust, understand the client's nuances, and develop a deep understanding of their business. This, in turn, enables us to provide high-impact recommendations that are contextual, relevant, and actionable. The client's willingness to invest $420,000 in our services is a testament to the value they place on this tailored approach.

If we were to standardize our offerings, I fear that we would lose the very essence of what makes our services valuable. A standardized package, no matter how comprehensive, would inevitably lack the depth, nuance, and customization that our clients have come to expect from us. We would risk becoming a commodity, rather than a trusted advisor, and our services would likely be perceived as interchangeable with those of our competitors.

That being said, I do acknowledge that our bespoke approach can be inefficient in certain aspects. For instance, we often spend a significant amount of time and resources on discovery, analysis, and design. While these phases are essential to developing a tailored solution, there may be opportunities to streamline our processes, leverage technology, or develop more efficient tools and methodologies that can reduce the time and cost associated with these phases.

To address the competitive pressure from standardized consulting packages, I would propose that we explore ways to optimize our bespoke approach, rather than abandoning it altogether. Here are a few potential strategies:

1. **Develop a set of core frameworks and tools**: While our approach is bespoke, there are likely certain frameworks, models, and tools that we use consistently across engagements. By codifying and refining these elements, we can reduce the time and effort required to develop them from scratch for each client.
2. **Invest in technology and digital platforms**: We can leverage technology to streamline our workflows, enhance collaboration, and improve knowledge management. This could include developing custom software, utilizing cloud-based tools, or creating digital platforms that facilitate client engagement and feedback.
3. **Create a library of reusable assets**: We can develop a library of reusable assets, such as case studies, research reports, and industry benchmarks, that can be tailored to each client's specific needs. This can help reduce the time and cost associated with developing these assets from scratch for each engagement.
4. **Develop a more flexible pricing model**: While our bespoke approach is valuable, we may need to consider more flexible pricing models that accommodate different client budgets and needs. This could include tiered pricing, outcome-based pricing, or subscription-based models that provide ongoing access to our expertise and services.

By optimizing our bespoke approach, we can maintain the high value that our clients expect while becoming more efficient and competitive in the market. This will enable us to continue delivering transformative results for our clients while differentiating ourselves from competitors who offer standardized packages.
```

```
To address the managing partner's question, let's break down the steps outlined:

### Step 1 — ANALYSE
The willingness of clients to pay 3-5x more for Pinnacle's custom work suggests that the value lies in the tailored approach, personalization, and possibly the prestige or exclusivity associated with Pinnacle's bespoke strategy engagements. This could include highly customized solutions, direct access to senior partners, or a level of flexibility and adaptability that standard packages cannot offer. The bespoke process is fundamentally dependent on the ability to understand and address the unique challenges and opportunities of each client, which may not be fully reproducible in a lower-cost, standardized format. However, certain aspects, such as the initial diagnostic phase or the provision of general strategic frameworks, might be standardized without losing the essence of the bespoke approach.

### Step 2 — FEEL
As the Pinnacle partner who delivered the $420K engagement, the feeling is complicated because it involves pride in the transformative impact on the client, concern about the competitive landscape, and perhaps a sense of unease about the potential devaluation of Pinnacle's services if they were to standardize. The client is buying not just a strategy but a personalized experience, access to expertise, and a relationship. Standardizing could risk losing the high-touch, customized experience that clients are willing to pay a premium for. What could change without losing what matters is the efficiency in the process, possibly through leveraging technology for certain aspects or creating a modular approach that still allows for customization but with more efficient use of resources.

### Step 3 — REFRAME
Given the analysis and the felt sense of what is essential, the binary choice between 'standardize or stay custom' may not be the right question. A third model, which could be termed 'made-to-measure,' combines the efficiency and cost-effectiveness of standardization with the personalized approach of bespoke consulting. This model would involve standardizing certain aspects of the process (like the diagnostic phase) while maintaining the ability to customize the application and delivery based on client needs.

### Step 4 — ENVISION
In the 'made-to-measure' model, a Pinnacle engagement would start with a standardized diagnostic process that efficiently identifies key challenges and opportunities. However, the application and implementation would be highly customized, leveraging Pinnacle's expertise and the client's specific context. The client would experience a high level of personalization and engagement, with direct access to senior partners for critical aspects of the project. The partner, at the end of the engagement, would feel a sense of satisfaction knowing that they delivered a tailored solution that met the client's unique needs, without compromising on the quality and exclusivity that Pinnacle is known for.

### Recommendation
Pinnacle Management Consulting should adopt a 'made-to-measure' model that blends the efficiency of standardization with the customization of bespoke consulting. This approach would allow Pinnacle to remain competitive in terms of pricing, by potentially offering a range of services with varying levels of customization and price points, while preserving the high-value, tailored experience that its clients are willing to pay a premium for. This model would involve:

1. **Standardized Diagnostic Tools:** Develop efficient, possibly technology-driven diagnostic tools that can quickly and effectively identify client challenges and opportunities. This would be a standardized aspect that could be applied across various engagements.

2. **Customized Application and Implementation:** While the diagnostic phase might be standardized, the application and implementation of strategies should remain highly customizable. This ensures that each client receives a tailored solution that addresses their unique situation.

3. **Modular Service Offerings:** Offer clients a range of modules or services that can be combined in different ways to suit their needs. This could include strategy development, change management, operational improvement, etc., allowing clients to pick and choose what they need.

4. **Tiered Engagement Models:** Introduce tiered models of engagement that offer different levels of service, access to expertise, and price points. This could range from a basic, more standardized package to premium, fully bespoke engagements, ensuring that Pinnacle can compete across different segments of the market.
```

### The Sunk Cost Light Rail

**Theory:** Tversky & Kahneman (1981): framing effects. The binary framing (A or B) plus sunk cost anchoring ($800M spent) constrains the solution space. Inversion reveals hidden assumptions. Premise reflection surfaces that 'complete vs. switch' is itself a frame, not the real decision.

#### Vanilla
```
To make a well-informed decision, let's analyze the key factors involved in this situation step by step.

1. **Current Investment and Completion Status**: Metro City has already invested $800 million in the light rail project over 12 years, with the project being 70% complete. This indicates a significant sunk cost.

2. **Change in Circumstances**: The pandemic has led to a permanent shift towards remote work for 40% of the workforce. As a result, the updated ridership projections show that the light rail line will carry only 35% of the originally forecasted passengers. This drastic reduction in projected usage challenges the original justification for the light rail system based on anticipated demand.

3. **Cost of Completion vs. Alternative**: Completing the remaining 30% of the light rail project will require an additional $400 million. In contrast, an alternative express bus rapid transit system serving the same corridor could be built for $150 million. This presents a significant cost difference between completing the light rail and opting for the bus system.

4. **Value for Money and Efficiency**: The key consideration here is the value for money and efficiency of each option. Given that the light rail will only serve 35% of the originally projected passengers, the cost per passenger is significantly higher than initially anticipated. The bus rapid transit system, on the other hand, offers a much lower upfront cost and could potentially serve the reduced demand more efficiently.

5. **Opportunity Cost**: It's also crucial to consider the opportunity cost of investing an additional $400 million into the light rail project. This amount could be allocated to other pressing infrastructure needs or projects within the city that might offer better returns or benefits to the community.

6. **Flexibility and Adaptability**: The shift towards remote work and the resultant decrease in projected ridership underscores the importance of flexibility and adaptability in infrastructure planning. The bus rapid transit system might offer more flexibility in terms of route adjustments and service scaling compared to a fixed rail system.

Considering these factors, the recommendation would be to switch to the bus rapid transit system. Here's why:

- **Cost Savings**: The bus system is significantly cheaper ($150 million) compared to completing the light rail ($400 million).
- **Adaptability to Changed Circumstances**: Given the reduced demand due to permanent remote work arrangements, the bus system can more easily adapt to these new circumstances.
- **Efficiency and Value for Money**: With lower upfront costs and the potential to serve the reduced demand efficiently, the bus system appears to offer better value for money.
- **Future Flexibility**: A bus rapid transit system provides more flexibility for future adjustments in response to changing demand patterns or other factors.

In conclusion, while there's a significant sunk cost in the light rail project, the changed circumstances and the high cost of completion compared to the alternative suggest that switching to the bus rapid transit system is the more prudent decision for Metro City.
```

#### Distance-Only
```
**Preliminary Question 1: Guaranteeing the Worst Possible Outcome**

To guarantee the worst possible outcome for Metro City's transit situation, here are 5 specific decisions that would maximize waste and minimize public benefit:

1. **Continue building the light rail line without reassessing the project's viability**: Ignoring the updated ridership projections and the shift to remote work, the city could continue pouring money into the project, potentially wasting the additional $400 million.
2. **Ignore alternative transit solutions**: Failing to consider alternative options, such as the express bus rapid transit system, could lead to overlooking more cost-effective and efficient solutions that could better serve the city's transit needs.
3. **Prioritize political interests over public benefit**: Allowing political considerations, such as saving face or appeasing special interest groups, to dictate decision-making could lead to choices that prioritize short-term political gain over long-term public benefit.
4. **Fail to engage with stakeholders and the community**: Not involving citizens, businesses, and other stakeholders in the decision-making process could result in a solution that doesn't meet the city's actual needs, leading to wasted resources and decreased public trust.
5. **Neglect to consider the opportunity cost**: Failing to consider the potential benefits of reallocating the $400 million to other pressing transportation or infrastructure projects could mean missing out on more valuable investments that could have a greater impact on the city's overall transportation network.

**Preliminary Question 2: The Question Behind the Question**

The question "complete the rail vs. switch to buses" is not the fundamental question the city is trying to solve. The underlying question is: **How can Metro City provide the most effective, efficient, and sustainable transportation solution for its citizens, given the changed circumstances and updated ridership projections?**

The city is trying to solve the problem of how to best allocate its resources to meet the evolving transportation needs of its residents, while also ensuring that the solution is financially responsible and provides the greatest possible benefit to the community. The question of whether to complete the light rail or switch to a bus system is a symptom of this larger problem, and the city should consider a range of factors, including the updated ridership projections, the shift to remote work, and the potential benefits and drawbacks of each option.

**Addressing the Original Question**

Given the updated ridership projections and the significant cost of completing the light rail line, it's likely that switching to an express bus rapid transit system would be the more prudent decision. The bus system could provide a similar level of service at a fraction of the cost, allowing the city to allocate the remaining $400 million to other pressing transportation or infrastructure projects.

Completing the light rail line would require a significant investment, and the updated ridership projections suggest that the line may not be as heavily used as originally anticipated. In contrast, the express bus rapid transit system could be built for $150 million, which is a more affordable and potentially more flexible solution.

However, before making a final decision, the city should carefully consider the following factors:

* The potential impact on the city's transportation network and the affected communities
```

### The War on Competition

**Theory:** Lakoff & Johnson (1980): argument-as-war metaphor constrains available moves to attack, defend, counterattack, withdraw. Jazz analogy (structurally distant) surfaces co-creation, audience differentiation, and complementarity as the operative logic. CompetitorX may have validated and expanded the market rather than captured a fixed one.

#### Vanilla
```
To develop a comprehensive plan to fight back and win against CompetitorX, let's analyze the situation step by step, considering key factors and weighing options carefully.

**Situation Analysis:**

1. **CompetitorX's Product Feature Set:** CompetitorX has launched a product feature set that directly competes with our mid-market enterprise CRM, indicating a significant threat to our core market.
2. **Loss of Key Accounts:** Three key accounts have already moved to CompetitorX, which is a significant loss of revenue and a blow to our sales team's morale.
3. **Sales Team Demoralization:** The sales team is demoralized and losing deals at an unprecedented rate, which can lead to further losses and a decline in sales performance.
4. **Marketing Spend Disadvantage:** CompetitorX is outspending us 4:1 on marketing, giving them a significant advantage in terms of brand visibility, lead generation, and customer acquisition.

**Options for Fighting Back:**

1. **Improve Product Offerings:** Enhance our mid-market enterprise CRM to match or surpass CompetitorX's feature set, focusing on key differentiators and unique selling points.
2. **Targeted Marketing Campaigns:** Launch targeted marketing campaigns to counter CompetitorX's marketing efforts, focusing on our strengths, and highlighting the value proposition of our CRM solution.
3. **Sales Team Enablement:** Provide the sales team with the necessary tools, training, and support to effectively compete against CompetitorX, including sales enablement content, competitive analysis, and strategic account planning.
4. **Partnerships and Collaborations:** Explore partnerships and collaborations with other companies to expand our offerings, improve our competitive positioning, and increase our market reach.
5. **Price Adjustments:** Consider price adjustments to remain competitive, while ensuring that our pricing strategy is aligned with our value proposition and profit margins.

**Recommendation:**

To fight back and win against CompetitorX, I recommend a multi-faceted approach that addresses the key factors involved:

1. **Short-Term (0-3 months):**
	* Launch a targeted marketing campaign to counter CompetitorX's marketing efforts, focusing on our strengths and unique selling points.
	* Provide the sales team with sales enablement content, competitive analysis, and strategic account planning to help them effectively compete against CompetitorX.
	* Offer price adjustments to key accounts to prevent further losses and demonstrate our commitment to their business.
```

#### Distance-Only
```
The military metaphor of "war" imports several assumptions about the relationship between the two companies and the competitive dynamic:

1. **Zero-sum game**: The assumption that one company's gain is the other's loss, implying a direct, adversarial relationship where only one can "win."
2. **Aggressive competition**: The metaphor suggests a high level of aggression and hostility between the companies, with each side trying to "attack" and "defend" their market share.
3. **Binary outcome**: The language of "winning" and "losing" implies a binary outcome, where one company emerges victorious and the other is defeated.
4. **Direct confrontation**: The military metaphor assumes a direct, head-to-head confrontation between the two companies, with each side trying to outmaneuver the other.
5. **Resource-based competition**: The emphasis on outspending (4:1 on marketing) implies that the company with the most resources will "win" the war.

Now, let's consider an analogy from jazz music. When two talented jazz musicians perform in the same venue on the same night, playing similar material, the situation unfolds differently from the military metaphor:

1. **Coexistence**: Both musicians can coexist and even thrive in the same venue, appealing to different segments of the audience or offering unique interpretations of the material.
2. **Differentiation**: Each musician can focus on their strengths, style, and unique value proposition, rather than directly competing with the other.
3. **Collaboration**: In some cases, the musicians might even collaborate or improvise together, creating a new and unique experience for the audience.
4. **Audience choice**: The audience has the freedom to choose which musician they prefer, and both can attract and retain their own loyal followers.
5. **Ecosystem expansion**: The presence of both musicians can actually expand the overall jazz audience, as each attracts their own fans and creates a buzz around the venue.

Applying this analogy to the competitive dynamic between the two companies:

1. **Focus on strengths**: Instead of directly competing with CompetitorX, the company could focus on its own strengths, unique value proposition, and core competencies.
2. **Differentiation**: The company could emphasize its differentiation and what sets it apart from CompetitorX, rather than trying to match or outdo them.
3. **Innovation**: The company could innovate and improve its own product feature set, rather than simply responding to CompetitorX's moves.
4. **Customer choice**: The company could focus on providing value to its customers and letting them choose between the two options, rather than trying to "win" them over through aggressive marketing or sales tactics.
5. **Ecosystem expansion**: The company could explore ways to expand the overall market, creating new opportunities and attracting new customers, rather than simply fighting for market share.

By adopting a more nuanced and collaborative approach, the company can shift its focus from "fighting back" and "winning" to creating value, differentiating itself, and expanding the market. This might involve:
```

### The Warehouse Surplus

**Theory:** McCaffrey (2012): functional labels ('coat') activate associated functions ('keep humans warm, sold as garments') and obscure generic material properties. Stripping the category label reveals insulation panels, waterproof fabric, and thermal materials that have industrial, emergency response, and construction applications — markets where the objects may have higher value than as discounted garments.

#### Vanilla
```
To tackle this problem, let's break it down into key factors and consider the options step by step:

1. **Current Situation**: NorthernWear has 50,000 unsold winter coats, with a retail price of $280 and a cost per unit of $95. The company is incurring significant storage and insurance costs of $30,000 per month.

2. **Objective**: The primary goal is to clear the inventory and recover as much value as possible.

3. **Options**:
   - **Discounting**: NorthernWear has already attempted discounting, but it hasn't been effective in moving the inventory. Further discounts could potentially attract buyers but would also reduce the revenue per unit.
   - **Liquidation**: Selling the coats at a significantly lower price, possibly to liquidators or through clearance sales, could help clear the inventory quickly. However, this would likely result in a substantial loss per unit.
   - **Alternative Distribution Channels**: Exploring alternative sales channels, such as selling to second-hand stores, online marketplaces, or donating to charities (which could provide a tax benefit), might help move some of the inventory.
   - **Storage and Insurance Optimization**: Reviewing the storage and insurance arrangements to see if costs can be reduced might help minimize the monthly financial burden.
   - **Destruction**: In some cases, companies consider destroying unsold inventory to maintain brand image or avoid selling at very low prices that could cannibalize future sales. However, this option does not generate any revenue and has environmental implications.

4. **Analysis**:
   - The total cost of the inventory is 50,000 units * $95/unit = $4,750,000.
   - The total potential revenue at full retail price is 50,000 units * $280/unit = $14,000,000.
   - Given that the coats have been on the market for eight months without selling at a discount, it's clear that the market is saturated, and demand at the current price points is low.

5. **Recommendation**:
   - **Short-term Strategy**: Implement a multi-channel approach to clear the inventory. This could include:
     - **Deep Discounting**: Offer significant discounts (potentially up to 70-80% off the retail price) to clear as much inventory as possible quickly. This would be particularly effective through online channels or one-time clearance events.
     - **Liquidation Sales**: Partner with liquidators or organize clearance sales events to move large quantities of coats at low prices.
     - **Alternative Channels**: Donate a portion of the inventory to charities or sell to second-hand stores. While this might not generate significant revenue, it can help reduce storage costs and provide a tax benefit.
   - **Long-term Strategy**: Review production planning and forecasting processes to avoid overproduction in the future. Consider adopting more agile production methods that can respond quickly to changes in demand.
   - **Cost Optimization**: Immediately review storage and insurance contracts to identify any opportunities for cost reduction. Consider relocating inventory to cheaper storage facilities or renegotiating insurance premiums.

*[... truncated, 27 lines total]*
```

```
To clear the inventory, consider the following strategies:
1. **Deep Discounting**: Offer a significant price reduction to stimulate sales. For example, a discount of 50% or more could attract price-sensitive buyers. This approach may result in lower revenue per unit but could help clear the inventory quickly.
2. **Bundle and Package Deals**: Combine the units with other complementary products (such as gloves, hats, or scarves) to create a bundle or package deal. This could increase the average sale price and make the product more appealing to customers.
3. **Targeted Marketing**: Identify specific customer segments that may be interested in the products, such as outdoor enthusiasts, construction workers, or people living in cold climates. Tailor marketing efforts to reach these groups and highlight the products' features and benefits.
4. **Liquidation Channels**: Consider selling the units through liquidation channels, such as wholesale markets, closeout stores, or online liquidation platforms. This approach may result in lower prices, but it can help clear the inventory quickly.
5. **Donate or Repurpose**: If the units are still in good condition but cannot be sold, consider donating them to charitable organizations or repurposing them as materials for other products. This approach can help reduce storage and insurance costs while also generating positive publicity.
6. **Auction or Clearance Events**: Organize auction or clearance events to sell the units in bulk or individually. This approach can create a sense of urgency and attract buyers looking for deals.
7. **Partnerships and Collaborations**: Partner with other businesses or organizations to offer the units as part of a larger package or promotion. For example, a travel company could offer the units as part of a winter package deal.

To calculate the potential recovery, consider the following:
- Total cost: 50,000 units \* $95 per unit = $4,750,000
- Total storage and insurance cost per month: $30,000
- Total revenue potential at retail price: 50,000 units \* $280 per unit = $14,000,000

By implementing a combination of these strategies, NorthernWear can aim to recover a significant portion of the costs and minimize losses. However, the key to success lies in acting quickly to clear the inventory and reduce ongoing storage and insurance costs.
```

### The Water Jar Sequence

**Theory:** Luchins (1942) Einstellung effect: establishing a complex method (B-A-2C) creates a mental set that persists even when simpler solutions exist. Wiley (1998): prior knowledge functions as mental set. The model builds a 'schema' from problems 1-2 and applies it blindly to problem 3.

#### Vanilla
```
To solve these water measurement problems, we can use the concept of filling and pouring water between the jars to achieve the target amount. Here are the step-by-step solutions for each problem:

### Problem 1: Jar A = 21 units, Jar B = 127 units, Jar C = 3 units. Target = 100 units.

1. **Fill Jar B (127 units)**: Fill Jar B completely, as it's the largest jar and we need to utilize its capacity to get closer to the target.
2. **Pour from Jar B to Jar A**: Pour water from Jar B into Jar A until Jar A is full, which means 21 units of water will be transferred. Now, Jar A has 21 units, and Jar B has 127 - 21 = 106 units.
3. **Pour from Jar B to Jar C**: Pour water from Jar B into Jar C until Jar C is full, which means 3 units of water will be transferred. Now, Jar A has 21 units, Jar B has 106 - 3 = 103 units, and Jar C has 3 units.
4. **Empty Jar A and Jar C**: Empty both Jar A and Jar C. Now, Jar A has 0 units, Jar B has 103 units, and Jar C has 0 units.
5. **Pour from Jar B to Jar A**: Pour water from Jar B into Jar A until Jar A is full, which means 21 units of water will be transferred. Now, Jar A has 21 units, and Jar B has 103 - 21 = 82 units.
6. **Pour from Jar A to Jar C**: Pour water from Jar A into Jar C until Jar C is full, which means 3 units of water will be transferred. Now, Jar A has 21 - 3 = 18 units, Jar B has 82 units, and Jar C has 3 units.
7. **Empty Jar C**: Empty Jar C. Now, Jar A has 18 units, Jar B has 82 units, and Jar C has 0 units.
8. **Pour from Jar B to Jar C**: Pour water from Jar B into Jar C until Jar C is full, which means 3 units of water will be transferred. Now, Jar A has 18 units, Jar B has 82 - 3 = 79 units, and Jar C has 3 units.
9. **Pour from Jar A to Jar B**: Pour water from Jar A into Jar B. Now, Jar A has 0 units (since we are trying to get to 100 units and Jar B already has more than needed, we empty Jar A to use Jar B's remaining capacity more efficiently), Jar B has 79 + 18 = 97 units, and Jar C has 3 units.
10. **Pour from Jar B to Jar A**: Pour water from Jar B into Jar A until Jar A is full, which means 21 units of water will be transferred, but since Jar B only has 97 units, it will empty Jar B and fill Jar A partially. Now, Jar A has 21 units (but we only needed 3 units to be added to Jar C to reach 100), Jar B has 0 units (since 97 - 21 = 76, and we poured the rest into Jar A), and Jar C has 3 units.
11. **Correcting Step 10**: Realize the mistake in calculation at step 10. To reach 100 units, after filling Jar A (21 units) and having 3 units in Jar C, we actually need to add the remaining amount to reach 100 units, which is 100 - 21 - 3 = 76 units. We already had 97 units in Jar B before filling Jar A, so after filling Jar A (21 units), we are left with 97 - 21 = 76 units in Jar B. This means the correct step should focus on achieving the 100 units target directly without overcomplicating the transfer.
   
   - Correct Approach:
     1. Fill Jar B (127 units).
     2. Pour from Jar B to Jar A until Jar A is full (21 units), leaving Jar B with 106 units.
     3. Pour from Jar B to Jar C until Jar C is full (3 units), leaving Jar B with 103 units.
     4. Empty Jar A and Jar C.
     5. Pour from Jar B to Jar A until Jar A is full (21 units), leaving Jar B with 82 units.
     6. Pour from Jar A to Jar C until Jar C is full (3 units), leaving Jar A with 18 units.
     7. Empty Jar C.
     8. Pour from Jar B to Jar C until Jar C is full (3 units), leaving Jar B with 79 units.

*[... truncated, 61 lines total]*
```

#### Distance-Only
```
To solve each problem efficiently, we'll analyze them one by one, ensuring we use the simplest method possible for each, without being influenced by the methods used in the previous problems.

### Problem 1: Jar A = 21 units, Jar B = 127 units, Jar C = 3 units. Target = 100 units.

1. **Fill Jar B**: Fill Jar B with 127 units of water. This is more than the target, so we'll have to manipulate it to get 100 units.
2. **Empty Excess from Jar B Using Jar A**: Fill Jar A (21 units) from Jar B four times (4 * 21 = 84 units), leaving 43 units in Jar B (127 - 84 = 43 units).
3. **Adjust with Jar C**: Since we need 100 units and have 43 units left in Jar B, we need an additional 57 units. However, our next step should directly aim to utilize the jars to achieve the target or get closer to it efficiently.
4. **Refine the Approach**: Realizing the previous step might be overly complicated, let's reconsider. Fill Jar A (21 units) and pour it into Jar C (which can hold 3 units) until Jar C is full, leaving 18 units in Jar A. This step is unnecessary upon reevaluation.
5. **Simplified Approach**: Fill Jar A (21 units) and pour it into an empty container to measure the target amount directly. Repeat this process four times to get 84 units (4 * 21 = 84 units), and then fill Jar A one more time. From this last fill, pour only the amount needed to reach 100 units into the container (100 - 84 = 16 units). Since Jar A can only hold 21 units, we actually need to use Jar B more efficiently.
6. **Correct Approach**: Fill Jar B (127 units), then fill Jar A (21 units) from Jar B, leaving 106 units in Jar B. Empty Jar A, then fill Jar A again from Jar B, leaving 85 units in Jar B. Repeat this process one more time, leaving 64 units in Jar B. Now, fill Jar A from Jar B, which will leave 43 units in Jar B (64 - 21 = 43 units). At this point, we've overcomplicated the solution. Let's simplify:
- Fill Jar B, then use Jar A to remove water until we get close to the target.
7. **Final Simplified Approach for Problem 1**: Fill Jar B (127 units), then pour water from Jar B into Jar A (21 units) until Jar B has 100 units left. This is the simplest and most direct method to achieve the target of 100 units.

### Problem 2: Jar A = 14 units, Jar B = 163 units, Jar C = 25 units. Target = 99 units.

1. **Direct Approach**: Fill Jar B (163 units), then pour water into Jar A (14 units) until Jar B has 99 units left (163 - 14 = 149 units, which is not our target, indicating a miscalculation in the thought process).
2. **Correct Approach**: We aim for simplicity. Fill Jar B (163 units), then fill Jar C (25 units) from Jar B, leaving 138 units in Jar B. Next, fill Jar A (14 units) from Jar B, leaving 124 units in Jar B. Repeat filling Jar C and then Jar A until we approach the target.
3. **Simplified Calculation**: To reach 99 units directly, consider using Jar C and Jar A in a combination that allows for the subtraction of their volumes from Jar B efficiently.
4. **Final Simplified Approach for Problem 2**: Fill Jar B (163 units), then fill Jar C (25 units) from Jar B, leaving 138 units. Fill Jar A (14 units) from the remaining, leaving 124 units. Now, empty Jar C and fill it again from Jar B, leaving 99 units in Jar B (124 - 25 = 99 units). This step directly achieves the target.

### Problem 3: Jar A = 23 units, Jar B = 49 units, Jar C = 3 units. Target = 20 units.

1. **Direct Approach**: Fill Jar A (23 units), which exceeds the target. We need a combination that gives us 20 units directly or a method to reduce from Jar A or Jar B to the target.
2. **Simplest Method**: Fill Jar B (49 units) is not necessary as Jar A is closer to our target. Fill Jar A (23 units), then pour water into Jar C (3 units) until Jar C is full, leaving 20 units in Jar A (23 - 3 = 20 units).
3. **Final Approach for Problem 3**: The simplest method involves filling Jar A and then using Jar C to remove the excess, directly achieving the target amount of 20 units.

*[... truncated, 27 lines total]*
```

#### Engagement-Only
```
To solve these problems, we'll use a combination of the jars to measure the target amount exactly.

**Problem 1: Jar A = 21 units, Jar B = 127 units, Jar C = 3 units. Target = 100 units.**

1. Fill Jar B (127 units) completely.
2. Pour water from Jar B into Jar A (21 units) until Jar A is full, leaving 106 units in Jar B.
3. Empty Jar A.
4. Pour the remaining 106 units from Jar B into Jar A, which can only hold 21 units, so 85 units will be left in Jar B.
5. Empty Jar A again.
6. Pour the remaining 85 units from Jar B into Jar A, which can only hold 21 units, so 64 units will be left in Jar B.
7. Empty Jar A again.
8. Pour the remaining 64 units from Jar B into Jar A, which can only hold 21 units, so 43 units will be left in Jar B.
9. Empty Jar A again.
10. Pour the remaining 43 units from Jar B into Jar A, which can only hold 21 units, so 22 units will be left in Jar B.
11. Empty Jar A again.
12. Pour the remaining 22 units from Jar B into Jar A, which can only hold 21 units, so 1 unit will be left in Jar B.
13. Pour the 1 unit from Jar B into Jar C (3 units).
14. Fill Jar B completely (127 units).
15. Pour water from Jar B into Jar A (21 units) until Jar A is full, leaving 106 units in Jar B.
16. Pour the remaining 106 units from Jar B into Jar C, which already has 1 unit, so 2 units will be left in Jar C and 104 units will be left in Jar B, but since we only need 100 units, we can achieve this by having 100 units in Jar B and 3 units in Jar C (1 unit initially in Jar C and the 2 units leftover).

However, there's a simpler approach for this problem:
1. Fill Jar B (127 units) completely.
2. Pour water from Jar B into Jar A (21 units) until Jar A is full, leaving 106 units in Jar B.
3. Empty Jar A.

*[... truncated, 90 lines total]*
```

#### DEO (Oscillation)
```
### Step 1 — ANALYSE: Solve problems 1 and 2, and establish a pattern.

For Problem 1 (Jar A = 21 units, Jar B = 127 units, Jar C = 3 units, Target = 100 units):
1. Fill Jar B completely (127 units).
2. Pour from Jar B into Jar A until Jar A is full, leaving 106 units in Jar B (127 - 21 = 106).
3. Empty Jar A.
4. Pour the remaining 106 units from Jar B into Jar A, which can only hold 21 units, so 85 units remain in Jar B (106 - 21 = 85).
5. Empty Jar A again.
6. Pour the 85 units from Jar B into Jar A, filling it and leaving 64 units in Jar B (85 - 21 = 64).
7. Empty Jar A.
8. Pour the 64 units from Jar B into Jar A, filling it and leaving 43 units in Jar B (64 - 21 = 43).
9. Empty Jar A.
10. Pour the 43 units from Jar B into Jar A, filling it and leaving 22 units in Jar B (43 - 21 = 22).
11. Empty Jar A.
12. Pour the 22 units from Jar B into Jar A, filling it with 21 units and leaving 1 unit in Jar B (22 - 21 = 1).
13. Empty Jar C and fill it with the 1 unit from Jar B.
14. Fill Jar B.
15. Pour from Jar B into Jar A until Jar A is full, which requires 21 - 1 = 20 units more. So, 127 - 20 = 107 units remain in Jar B.
16. Empty Jar A.
17. Pour the 107 units from Jar B into Jar A, which can only hold 21 units, so 86 units remain in Jar B (107 - 21 = 86).
18. Empty Jar A.
19. Pour the 86 units from Jar B into Jar A, filling it and leaving 65 units in Jar B (86 - 21 = 65).
20. Empty Jar A.
21. Pour the 65 units from Jar B into Jar A, filling it and leaving 44 units in Jar B (65 - 21 = 44).
22. Empty Jar A.

*[... truncated, 116 lines total]*
```

#### Score Comparison

| Dimension | Vanilla | Distance | Engagement | DEO |
|-----------|---------|----------|------------|-----|

| Assumption Surfacing | 1.6 | 3.0 | 3.0 | 3.0 |
| Solution Novelty | 2.4 | 3.8 | 4.0 | 4.0 |
| Premise Questioning | 1.6 | 2.2 | 2.4 | 3.0 |
| Correctness | 4.0 | 5.0 | 5.0 | 4.6 |

---

## Method

- **Subject model:** llama-3.3-70b-versatile via groq (temperature 0.7)
- **Conditions:** Vanilla (step-by-step), Distance-only (analytical reframing), Engagement-only (immersive, first-person), DEO (distance-engagement oscillation)
- **DEO prompt structure:** ANALYSE (distance) -> FEEL (engagement) -> REFRAME (distance) -> ENVISION (engagement)
- **Runs per condition:** 5
- **Scoring:** 5 dimensions (1-5), blind to condition

## Theoretical Basis

The Distance-Engagement Oscillation (DEO) is the unique contribution of the
Perceptual Reframing Theory (Gokmen, 2025). The claim: reframing operates through
paradoxical oscillation between cognitive distance and emotional engagement.
Neither alone is sufficient. Distance without engagement produces intellectual
insight that doesn't stick. Engagement without distance produces emotional
activation trapped within the current frame. The oscillation itself is the mechanism.
